		<username>neo_dies</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Watch it</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Darjeeling Limited is a film that I will gladly recommend to almost everyone to watch. To summarize the film, it's about three brothers who haven't seen each other in one year, and who are brought together by Francis (Owen Wilson) for a self-defined "transformational spiritual journey". The idea is naturally absurd, that the "spirituality" of the journey is the point of it. He further tries to create transcendence in a bottle with "itineraries" printed daily that schedule trips to temples and shrines. Francis is sadly genre- savvy, and this causes the failure of the journey- the three brothers quibble and quarrel over everything. No one fully trusts both of the others, and all three mercilessly back-stab and betray the others. However, little merriment is derived from this - all three actors are deadpan, and speak haltingly. This results in a movie told without words, and instead the story is given during the dead space. Most of the arguments are over possessions, such as sunglasses and belts and food. The journey that they are making is really the antithesis to any spiritual journey- instead, they become further mired in their earthly issues. During the trip, they use drugs, cigarettes, and alcohol to further avoid spirituality, and so they do, until they are booted off the train when a quibble becomes a quarrel becomes a brawl. It is then that their journey truly begins. They are lost in India, and it is then that they can find spirituality. In an attempt to save children who are drowning in a river, they forget any arguments they were having and run towards them, dropping their luggage heedlessly in the road. This symbolism is not by accident; they have begun to lose their trappings, their "baggage", and it is only then that they can find themselves and each other. The movie ends with very little reconciled, but all three are on a train again, having lost all their luggage except for their clothes, and for the first time in the film, they look at each other and smile.The film is a colorful movie- every shot frames at least one, often two or three colors in conjunction. A relatively simple palette of mostly primary colors contrasts with the stuff-filled trains that they ride; every object on the train is painted with false opulence. The richness of color stays throughout the film, well into their visit to rural India. It lends a magical feel to the movie, a feel that in India, everything is brighter. The cinematography often centers on the three men sitting side by side in passivity, often with cigarettes hanging from their mouths and tumblers in hand, the very image of just not caring. This also changes towards the end, and due to the actor's talent, they can change just sitting to sitting with purpose and poise. All three of them are greatly underrated (yes, even Oscar-winner Adrien Brody).In conclusion, the movie is truly brilliant. You will feel changed and moved by it, and in fact I made this account just to review it. It will enchant you and hold your imagination, and you will not do anything for the rest of the night but think about it.9/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>wynonasbigbrownbeaver</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>The scenery was the biggest star of this movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>From what I have gathered from Anderson's Royal Tenenbaums and Life Aquatic, I figured The Darjeeling Limited would be another deadpan cookie cutter since many of choices frequently involve similar actors in 2 or more of his films. I would hate to admit that this movie did grow on me midway through.The casting of the three "brothers" Wilson, Brody, and Schwartzman actually worked as they gossip, fight and bond through this interesting "spiritual" journey. Anderson meticulously unravels each of their personalities with detail and intrigue without too much exposition thrown in all at once. The scenery of India and its environment was very appeasing to watch with the different types of people in this huge conglomerate of culture. Anderson throws in his occasional trademark diagonal close ups in certain shots to give it that grind house 70's look. The Indian natives acted perfectly natural and nothing seemed pre-staged in the way they go about their day to day lives.Angelica Huston has a cameo in the end as well as a frequently used Bill Murray while neither were all that awe inspiring. The spiritual journey doesn't really mark anything in the end and is a bit confusing to what the movie was trying to accomplish as far as a moral other than the unity of the trio. The rather open ending was nonetheless the downfall due a lack of climax. I am still trying to wonder about the justifications on why the trio continue to ride the trains though there are more important matters in their lives that they should attend to. (Spoiler) Why would Brody's character want to remain bound to his brother when his wife is close to giving birth? The movie does continue to be complacent in its execution without any justification.I recommend this movie for any Anderson fan though it isn't for everyone. The scenery and the chemistry between the three brothers are probably the movie's biggest strengths.</fullReview>

		<username>planktonrules</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Obviously the filmmakers seemed to care less if you like the film--some will adore it and many more will feel baffled why anyone would watch such a film.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Three brothers (Jason Schwartzman, Adrien Brody and Owen Wilson) are on a LONG trip through India, though for about half the film you have no idea why they are there. In fact, they spend their time snipping at each other and seem to have nothing in common. They seem disconnected and rather self-absorbed. Only late in the film do you learn that they have come to the country to search for their mother--a mother who abandoned the family some time ago to 'serve God' and help others. Along the journey, they mostly reveal that they are rather soulless jerks--though there are some positive aspects to the journey (such as when one nearly dies trying to save a child). My assumption about the men is that are supposed to be soulless, mixed up and disconnected as a result of their mother abandoning them....or not.Like most Wes Anderson films I have seen, this one does NOT appear to have been written with any intention of making any money at all. Instead, his quirky films seem designed to please Anderson and his friends (who appear in his films again and again) as well as to impress many of the critics and lovers of artsy films. However, I can guarantee that the average person would NOT like "The Darjeeling Limited", as it seems to meander too much, has little in the way of conventional plot AND because it seems like a comedy with no punchlines. In recent years, Anderson as well as Sophia Coppola ("Lost in Translation") and Jim Jarmusch ("Broken Flowers") have produced very similar films (ones, incidentally that have Bill Murray in them)--films that have practically no commercial appeal and which seem to just meander. Some adore these films and some hate them (read through the reader reviews--you'll rarely see such divergent reviews on a film than these). Me, I like SOME (such as Anderson's recent "Moonrise Kingdom"), but can't see the point to many of them. And, as for "The Darjeeling Limited", I see glimpses of something I like but that is all--such as a scene here or a scene there. In fact, the film is super-frustrating as just when I think I'm starting to like it I realize, no, I do NOT!</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I wanted to like this movie...really I did. Having sat through this terrible movie, I can understand why their mother ran away from them. They spend the entire journey arguing like kids and being stupid, whilst the sumptuous colours and sights of the Indian sub-continent slide beautifully by. The script is agonisingly banal and the characters are shallow; it's impossible to feel anything other than antagonism towards them. I have rarely felt such antipathy towards characters before, but by the 30 minute mark I was ready to throw shoes at them. It's not just that I found them annoying but also because I was losing the will to live. But I soldiered gamely on until the end in the name of stubbornness. Owen Wilson was once again execrable and confirmed my belief that he cannot act at all, while Adrian Brody at least has a body of mostly excellent work to prove his credentials. It's a bad movie, don't waste your time with it. Watch Rushmore or The Royal Tennenbaums instead. This is a complete dud.</fullReview>

Siblings often know each other so well, and yet they never really know each other well enough, as is the truth with these three brothers. Still grieving over the death of their father, they argue and come together over and over again. They confront their mother along the way and each other about the events that followed the death of their father. When it is all said and done, no three brothers can come together and find such a spiritual bond as these three. I cried, I laughed, and I fell in love with the music. 		<shortDescription>Symbolism does not a good movie make</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm familiar with Wes Anderson's work, though I've never been a rabid fan at all. This film was too steeped in it's own aspirations to be intelligent and full of symbolism, that it forgot to do what movies are meant to do - entertain. If you're going to make a film that is full of symbolism which you'll only really pick up on during repeat viewings, then ensure your film is worth watching more than once! I watched the film with a friend, who fell asleep 15 minutes into it. I managed to make it all the way through, but toward the end I was on the PC and looking up explanations of the symbolism in the film, because otherwise, there is no point. I didn't feel like thinking about the movie too much, because the characters were so droll and thinly painted, it just didn't keep my interest.It doesn't help when the movie is constantly slapping false emotion in your face with blaring, "quirky" music and slow motion. That does NOT help get a point across. In my case, it simply annoyed me.There were one or two moments in the film where something actually happened, and I was entertained for a split second... But then it collapsed back into overly long scenes of dry, inane dialogue. The performances were bland, and it really didn't seem as though anyone was into the story at all - even the director. I'm sure the idea sounded smart on paper, but the execution was incredibly weak.Yes, the art design and cinematography were interesting to look at - that is where the film succeeded. When you take the symbolism into account, there IS potential for an interesting story in there somewhere, but as it is, it's hidden amongst a bunch of boring dialogue and (some subtle, some blatant) visual cues. I'll give it a 5/10 because the idea was there - too bad the execution was so soulless. In reality it probably deserves a 4...</fullReview>

		<username>gary-pope</username>
		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Not moving, not profound, not clever</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'd hazard a guess that the only method involved in the making of this shocker was Anderson's crew and actors settling themselves down in Rajasthan for a couple of months with no apparent plot or cohesive dialogue, filming the ensuing wacky shenanigans then crossing their fingers and hoping that a meaningful film would result. That's certainly how it appears anyway.I'm a big admirer of (and have on DVD) Anderson's previous efforts, as well as being an old India hand, so I was really looking forward to this. However, I found it a crushing disappointment and the director has some real work to do on his next outing if his reputation is to recover from this absolute train wreck of a film. Other reviewers have stated that you either love or hate Anderson's films (presumably to rationalise the many bad reviews); well that maybe the case to some people, but not myself. I loved Rushmore, Tenenbaums and Acquatic but hate this one with a passion. In a year or so, Darjeeling will correctly be regarded as the juvenile, vacuous, incoherent, patronising excuse for a movie that it is. What is astonishing me are the positive reviews it seems to be getting from many on here and in the media. Is it that the - sometimes spectacular and mystical - Indian setting is conning some punters into believing that they're actually watching something moving and profound? (Just as the Andes-based "Motorcycle Diaries" did to many).The handout I received in the theatre stated that Anderson credits the films of Satayjit Ray as a being major inspiration for TDJ. Just how does that influence come through? Let's see: 1) It's a film 2) it's set in India 3) Er...that's it. Unfortunately this film has more in common with "Carry On Up the Kyber" (a far superior work) than the Apu Trilogy or The Music Room. Anderson's film proves conclusively that Americans just don't understand India. The director's "middle-class, disturbed, quirky family" premise has well and truly run it's course with this effort and it's high time for him to move on. I rate this just below "You, Me and Dupree" as worst film of the year. Excellent job, Owen Wilson!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Fanaticalaboutfilms Review: The Darjeeling Limited</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Darjeeling Limited is about 3 brothers Francis (Owen Wilson), Peter (Adrien Brody) and Jack (Jason Schwartzman) who have not seen each other since their father's funeral over a year ago. The death of their father has affected them all individually and along with other problems in their life has left them somewhat flawed and drugged up. Peter has girlfriend issues, Jack has father issues and Francis has family issues, he wants to be closer to his brothers and be a happy family and yet his demeanour drives them away.The lack of seeing each other inspires Francis to bring them together so they can carry out a 'spiritual journey' across India on a train in order to rekindle their relationships with one another become closer and more brotherly. However this idea does not go completely to plan with much brotherly bickering and fighting, the involvement of some pepper spray and one of the brothers buying a highly poisonous snake which he then misplaces amongst some of the things to look forward to.The brothers and their interactions and chemistry with each other is where this movie wins from my point of view. Despite showing hardly any emotion- they never seem sad or cry, rarely smile etc. they convey what they are feeling in different ways. Having only ever seen Wilson in the bumbling comic/romcom type role, I was impressed with him in this where he shows off his talents as the brother who needs to always be in control. Brody also impresses with Schwartzman, despite not quite being as good as the other brothers still performing admirably.The shooting of some of the scenes in the train reminded me in principal of similar work in the movie The Life Aquatic with Steve Zissou whereby the camera moves alongside the train cutting between rooms as if the nearest wall to the camera did not exist (I've heard it called the dollhouse effect?). This similarity was explained when I discovered both movies were directed by the same man, Wes Anderson.These types of shots could be achieved due to The Darjeeling Limited being filmed inside a moving train- Anderson rented a ten carriage train and locomotive engine, completely redecorated it and then ran it between Jodhpur and Jaisalmer on a live section of railway track. This unsurprisingly providing a great challenge for cinematographer Robert D. Yeoman as nothing could be fixed to the ceiling and filming equipment couldn't be more than a metre out of the windows. He does a fantastic job though which has to be highly commended.This is not an 'in your face' comedy but is rather more sly, subtle and witty one which I preferred in this context. There are some great one liners that I particularly enjoyed. The characters make it what it is while dragging their emotional (and actual) baggage around with them for the whole of the movie. If you like Anderson's other movies then this will not disappoint either.For further reviews feel free to check out: http://www.fanaticalaboutfilms.com</fullReview>

		<username>chaos-rampant</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Spiritual shenanigans</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The first half of this is strong, because of the devices that control it and which Wes Anderson adeptly executes.One is the consciousness of story folding and unfolding, where coincidence is a cosmic joke (such as when Bill Murray in the beginning ostensibly misses the train of the movie) and the narrative is experienced by the characters as the capricious whims of a higher decree. When the train for example has to make a stop in the middle of the desert to orientate (itself the source of metaphorical amusement) and Owen Wilson is told that "they need to locate themselves", Anderson quickly points out the obvious symbolism.Here, perhaps more profoundly than anywhere else in the movie, I feel the hand of the director as the hand of god. The more the characters try to effect control over their lives, the more he wrestles it away and tosses them around. Owen Wilson's onanistic desire for a spiritual journey Wes Anderson denies or taunts, incorporating instead the sought after symbolic dimension as a quip within the film.The other is the mock spiritual journey embarked upon by the three brothers, which happens irreverently, indifferently, with mock devoutness or none at all, among consumption of copious amounts of painkillers, whiskey, and shenanigans involving deadly snakes and bathroom sex, and which, by circumstances out of their control and by the filmmaker's higher decree, threatens to turn into a real spiritual journey. Anderson divines this with a solemn scene of death, and the subsequent funeral.But where does he go from here, how does he chronicle the change in the lives of these people and to what effect?The answer for me is that he doesn't, he's not equipped for it. Naturally the three of them learn to be whole again, in a touch of humane but formulaic optimism.The experience of the spiritual journey then is more prefabricated than the mock escapade it follows. The fallacy on the filmmaker's part rests on the belief that the usual tics and mannerisms, which are ornamental in style, decorational, can assist closure. In order to make the train in the end, the three brothers need to let go of the suitcases of their dead father (a presence that looms over the film). This equally shameless symbolic touch, which earlier was a source of ironic amusement, now Anderson wants us to experience in sentimental earnest.The two moments where the film is elevated, both hinge on inscrutable feelings.The one, when the three brothers, now reunited with their mother in the Himalayas, sit around a table saying with the eyes only all that needs or can be said. The other a little later, when back in a train station the three brothers make the same phonecalls, resuming their troubled lives as though nothing happened.The film's problem for me then is not that it suggests these people were changed in their escapade very little or none at all, this is a valid observation, but that the film didn't. But whereas the irony that shows these people trapped, estranged from one another, is executed with a certain complexity of narrative, the closure is altogether easy. Reflecting, living through an experience, is hardly required here, simply watching this will do.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The Darjeeling Limited</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Owen Wilson nearly looses his life in a motorcycle crash, and after somewhat recuperating orchestrates a spiritual journey on a train vacation in India with his estranged two brothers. His head and face covered in bandages, Wilson has an itinerary of what the three of them will do during their time together, but things soon do not go according to plan. Director Wes Anderson's camera is often accessing the surroundings of our three principles(Wilson, Adrien Brody, Jason Schwartzman)as they bicker, make up, bicker, make up along the trip through India experiencing various situations--fighting over secrets they keep from each other(Brody's wife is pregnant, Schwartzman's problems with a European lover and his return passport as a precautionary measure if things between the brothers got too hairy), trying to track down their mother who has since started a mission for the Lord somewhere(she's always abandoning them, even doing so when they finally find her, sneakily when they are asleep), coping with the loss of their father when he was hit by a taxi(and fussing about Brody's using all of pop's things such as his razor to shave or wearing his shades), the rescue of Indian boys who nearly drown while trying to cross a stream(Brody's kid isn't so lucky), Schwartzman's awkward sexual relations with an Indian train stewardess, bringing a dangerous snake on board and losing it in their room, etc. Anyone accustomed to Anderson's style knows the acting is reserved and very subtle(there's a reason why Owen Wilson is often a star in his movies) and he communicates to the viewer through visual methods(how he shoots over the shoulder when the brothers open their suitcases or how he pulls the camera back outside the train windows, following the characters from different cars, looking upon them from afar). Anderson seems interested in not just his main actors but what is happening around them, such as when they are among Indians in their village(and a commune where the three are invited after saving children from drowning)..I think this indicates that his movie just isn't about the men, but their experiences and the place for which they find themselves. I felt Anderson loves India and cares deeply about the people there. And, what I found most satisfying was that these brothers do bond, despite numerous obstacles which might threaten to disastrously halt Wilson's desire for the three of them to find a peace and love that is lasting and special. Anjelica Huston has a small part as the mother hiding away in a convent from her sons, reacting disappointingly when they question why she didn't attend their father's funeral. I didn't think THE DARJEELING LIMITED(which is the name of the train for which the brothers spend most of the first half of the film traveling on)was as successful as something like THE ROYAL TENENBAUMS(this film really spends most of its time with just the three central characters and their experiences, instead of the typical huge ensemble we are used to seeing in a Wes Anderson film)or THE LIFE AQUATIC, but seems more personal to the director. There's a sense of loss and mourning that permeates throughout the movie, the brothers having to deal with a missing father and mother, not to mention happiness and purpose. Both Bill Murray and Natalie Portman appear in cameos.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>An Indy film with a studio feel.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I liked this film. I enjoyed it. It had style and an artistic bent to it. But some things kept me from really enjoying it fully.Fist off were the three actors chosen to portray brothers. They're great actors, but they didn't resemble one another in the least. Maybe I'm being too picky, or maybe I'm missing some deeper inner meaning in the film, but it seemed a little artificial. It felt like a film made by independent film makers who, at heart, were really studio film makers, although they themselves don't realize it.Even so, I liked it. As far as the "road trip" genre goes this one strikes some reasonably good chords. The relationship is nurtured and grows through a confluence of disconnects and confrontations, each one leading to the other to reveal the characters true selves.Set against the backdrop of India I was very glad to see the film makers striving to take advantage of a very exotic part of the world, and to give the audience a taste for the location. But, I was disappointed that the culture was not fully embraced nor explored. It was touched on, but set almost as an off-screen character than active participant. To find out why you need to see the film yourself :-) A minor high was seeing the use of the quick zoom in this film. A thing rarely (if ever) used in contemporary cinema, and for good reason; it's cheesy. But its use here, though mildly off, works for the film. Another plus is the chief purser. Though an Indian Sikh he doesn't play the stereo typical Indian with a pointed Punjabi accent, nor is he apologetic. Even so he has not lost his cultural roots.The humor is somewhat bland and flat (as was the intent), but the laughs expected and implied by the film's trailer were simply not there. And that was a let down.The film staggers along at a brisk pace until a third of the way into act two, at which point some substance begins to trickle into the three brother's lives. Their introduction, and subsequent rediscovery, isn't what I would call a great Hallmark in cinema, but it is interesting to see how the film makers concocted and executed the ultimate tale.One gets the sense that the cast and crew really wanted to be on the edge with this film in a good way, but one couldn't help but get the feeling that they lacked the spiritual experience to do justice to this tale. Their technical experience is there, as is the basic art skill, but to make a film like this really soar beyond that which it's already achieved, there needed to be a few more "dramatic" moments for lack of a better term.I couldn't help but feel that Wim Wenders, Percy Aldon, or Richard Donner probably would've really sunk their teeth into this material, and take it the full distance it deserved in terms of spiritual themes. As it is now it's a respectable film, but it could've been a great deal more. Other films in this genre include "Fandango" and "Mystery Train". If you liked "The Darjeeling Limited", then give these other two films a go.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>It appears as if you missed the point</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This review does not completely apply to you, but works nonetheless. I think that you gave a very bad review to the film and you were completely unfair. Here is why...With regard to the film not being "...at all insightful about human nature" and Wes Anderson's not having any "substance" in terms of his career and the film I would like to disagree and offer support for my disagreement. I am not sure if the dissenters are familiar with Freudian psychology- the basics would do- but many modern personality theories are based off of Freud's work, especially his division of the human consciousness into three parts: the Ego, the Super Ego, and the Id. Owen Wilson is the Super Ego, Adrian Brody is the Ego, and Jason Schwartzman is the Id. The reason that this is at all relevant is because the film is completely focused on Freud's theory to such an extent that one of the only real criticism I can give to it is that it is nothing but human nature and substance and neglects adding aspects of the fantastic, which I understand to be Anderson's style for the most part. This seems to be the exact opposite of the criticisms offered on the boards and as far as I can tell the only legitimate- which is to say non-name-calling- critique offered thus far. This actually leads me to a bigger point which is, unless you can offer some meaningful contribution to the discussion other than "this was pointless" or "this movie sucked" you should keep your thoughts alone where they start until they gather together to form a criticism that is more than shallow. The movie was very good and had a point in that it was able to tell us a story about the human consciousness in a relatively easy to understand manner. This was a brilliant display of Freud shown through the story of an unhealthy human consciousness that is split after the tragic loss of the father and abandonment of the mother. To further emphasize the point Wes Anderson and co-directors literally had the mother abandon the brothers at the end of the film, and to make it even more perspicuous the brothers literally left the "baggage" of their father behind when they were trying to catch the train that literally contained their lives (their women, their children, their snake, etc). I don't know how they could have told a more meaningful and human story to be honest. Feel free to disagree with me on any of the points that I have made here, but please, if you do so, try to make the disagreement clear and substantive.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Even More Exasperating Than "The Life Aquatic"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If narcissistic self-indulgence is the cardinal sin of good film-making, then Wes Anderson is the Antichrist--except he's so darn quirky. Let's face it, when you walk into a Wes Anderson film (or anything involving Owen Wilson) you have to expect magnificent squirreliness. Whether they admit it or not, that's what die-hard Anderson acolytes truly crave.Unlike Tarantino's Death Proof, which is a ridiculous mess that doesn't work, The Darjeeling Limited is a ridiculous mess that does work, at least in the quirky world of Wes Anderson. Thus, it's somewhat silly for people of Indian descent, or anyone else for that matter, to be offended by this weird travel-romp epic. After all, this is not supposed to be the real India; this is Wes Anderson's quirky-world India. So you either avoid it or wallow in it. I chose the latter, and I am better for it.Okay, I admit I'm not the biggest Wes Anderson fan, although, I kinda liked those early, yet unpretentious, quirky little films Bottle Rocket and Rushmore. And the classic mockumentary about the making of The Royal Tenenbaums was a real hoot (included as an extra disc with the DVD package). That pseudo-documentary, ostensibly a behind-the-scenes look at film-making through the quirky eyes of Anderson, actually imparts great insight for those wondering: "What happened to Wes Anderson?"It's by far my favorite Wes Anderson movie, and should be listed on IMDb instead of Hotel Chevalier.In reality, I think both Anderson and Tarantino are geniuses. They are able to lead fantastic lifestyles because early in their careers they established themselves as auteurs capable of attracting a certain level of guaranteed box office and critical acclaim. As a result, the quirky director needs no clothes to acquire train-loads money, millions of quirky fans, and adoring starlets ready and willing to take off their clothes (see the luscious Natalie Portman in the Hotel Chevalier opus).</fullReview>

		<username>MisterWhiplash</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>another hip, sad, and rewarding journey from one of the most idiosyncratic of America's auteurs</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's always predictable what Wes Anderson will do, least to a point. We know his style by now, which is loaded with quick, self-conscious pans and whip-ups with the frame, the meticulous attention to detail of objects and things as in composition of the actors just like a pad of paper or a river, slow-motion during intense/emo moments, and a tale of family and/or love that is complicated by the shallowness and flaws of the characters. It's almost comforting to see an auteur like Anderson at work, because as you know what you'll be getting, it's never boring. And he's a weird master of oddball dialog ("How can a train be lost?"), as well as mixing a combination of awkward comedy and sincere, tragic drama. If it isn't any great film it at least is one of the most admirable, colorful independent films of the year.It's the tale of three brothers, estranged and each without trust for one another (this includes withholding info like Adrien Brody's soon-to-be child, and a belt, plus a passport, and the end of the trip being to meet their much more distanced-from nun mother played by Huston), who are on a train across India. Like The Royal Tenenbaums, Anderson's masterpiece, its about characters who have created little bubbles with their quirks and troubles, the chief one being that they can't seem to communicate very well with one another. At least, anyway, from Francis (Owen Wilson) to Jack and Peter (Schwartzmann and Brody). Soon, in road movie fashion, they get thrown off the train, and come under a circumstance that nearly throws off their whole journey, and rightfully so. One's never too concerned with plot; one probably keeps on watching because there's so much to take in with the three brothers, and how each actor puts in their personalities so convincingly that, along with the script, we know this is how they've been their whole lives. Among the three I'd say that Owen Wilson takes the cake. Head bandaged throughout the film and with a penchant for bossiness with an itinerary, it's a subtly tense part for someone of Wilson's gifts at deadpan and charm. He's even more of a tortured soul than he was in Tenenbaums, just a little more complex with relation to the passed-on father and mother, and he makes Francis into one of his most touching, and saddening and prescient, performances. Brody and Schwartzmann are also up to task, making even the moments where a scene or beat falls flat on the train amusing and strange.And back to the style- watching Anderson and his crew at work is, within the predictable strands of an Anderson flick, unpredictable, or unpredictably inventive. As mentioned, the use of slow-motion is extraordinary even in the conventional realm; we've all seen a scene where a character chases to catch a train. There are a few of these scenes in the Darjeeling Limited, but every time, backed behind by the exquisite, flowery music, it's effectively timed and some of the closest times the director has come to "pure" cinema, in a sort of sense. Particularly a scene when the brothers are walking to a funeral for a boy who died tragically (that, by the way, is a small gem of dramatic daring and a step forward following some genre confusion in Life Aquatic), and they're seen going in slow-mo like it's something religious. I also liked how Anderson skipped into the structure with the flashback to the car shop: it penetrates the psyche of the characters just a little more, aside from being a sad, momentary expression of the human spirit.As Anderson goes on to make more films, maybe his outlook might expand too past familial bewilderment comedies (there is more of a concern this time for the "other", albeit, like Renoir's the River, through Western eyes). If you're already a fan of flicks like Rushmore and Tenembaums, go see it, and if you like the actors all the better. It shouldn't disappoint. Though it likely won't turn on too many who already have dismissed his body of work.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>hall895</username>

		<shortDescription>An interesting journey</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Darjeeling Limited follows three brothers on a train journey across India. Suffice to say that in the course of their journey they will be faced with some complications. Things most definitely do not go according to the original plan. And it is in those times where the trip veers off its intended course that the movie is at its best. It's not really a comedy but the film certainly has some nice comedic moments. There are some good dramatic moments but I don't think one would label the film a great drama. It's a journey which more often than not is certainly interesting but which in the end leaves you wanting a little more. For all the great moments, and there are more than a few, it doesn't quite all add up to a spectacular finished product. It's a good movie which you feel could have been great but fell a little shy of that standard.Owen Wilson, Adrien Brody and Jason Schwartzman play the three Whitman brothers who having not seen one another for a year are now embarking on what Francis, the oldest of the brothers, intends to be a spiritual journey. Francis is obsessive-compulsive to the extreme and his brothers often find him to be more than a touch annoying. But, as played wonderfully by Wilson, Francis comes across as rather endearing. Annoying yes but in an endearing way. The fact that he goes through the whole movie with his head and face heavily bandaged due to a motorcycle accident probably doesn't hurt in making Francis a sympathetic figure. Brody's Peter and Schwartzman's Jack do not immediately appear to have as many obvious quirks as Francis, seeming to be much more "normal". But we will see that Peter and Jack certainly have their own issues to deal with as well. And while if you had to choose one Wilson would be the star of the show both Brody and Schwartzman do excellent work as well.When the journey inevitably veers from its spiritual course is when the movie shines. Seeing the way these three very unique individuals react to one another in trying circumstances is a treat. Some of these moments are wonderfully comedic while other moments are appropriately solemn and serious. This is a train ride full of surprises which allow the brothers to learn a great deal about themselves and in the end maybe about each other. The film does wander at times. For all the good moments there are other times where the film struggles to maintain momentum. And really there isn't much of a plot to speak of. But while it may be a relatively simple story it is generally an enjoyable one. It must be said that director Wes Anderson has crafted a film which certainly looks amazing. Whether it be the wonderful Indian exteriors or the brilliantly vibrant interiors of the train itself the film always looks terrific. And the three lead actors do the visuals justice, giving excellent performances in bringing this charming story to life. It's not a perfect film by any means. It's a movie you'll embrace more for its moments than for the final product as a whole. There is the nagging sense that it didn't quite all come together. But all in all the movie works. You could do a lot worse than to take a ride on The Darjeeling Limited.</fullReview>
	</review>

The brothers, we garner across the movie, have not spent much time together since leaving their dysfunctional childhood home after college. He is quite a painful character in the extent to which he goes to in trying to arrange his brothers to get this 'togetherness to recur.Schwartzman is perhaps the most endearing one. He is a writer but is unable to write originally. Is this since he had his failed romance? Or fathers death one year before? This 'dilemma' is not solved, meanwhile we are subjected to tidbits of his life, snapshots, in a written form (that we see recreated on film or during the course of the film) and we and the bros. are meant. Brody we learn is to be a new father, left his wife back home in America, and is struggling with this new phase of his life.Bill Murray pops up here in a funny cameo in 'Darjeeling?' Along with Wilson, he is another in the Wes Anderson 'family', appearing in three of his films.Sometimes I feel Wes Andersons films are like trying to compete at the Olympics in the 'most dysfunctional family' event. Like 'Oh, we beat the Simpsons, the Tenenbaums (and now the Whitmans - is that a pun or a 'homage' to the highly regarded American author?)'. 'The Darjeeling Limited' is an okay flick to see if you want a bit of fun, fluffy feelings, sightseeing, maybe a little cry, you might feel good after it, and see some of India too (shot on location). You might feel ill too. Enjoy.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Three Lost souls swimming in a fish bowl</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As many people who have written comments on this film have said, I do believe that Wes Anderson is one of the most most important American filmmakers of his generation, however, not many other decent filmmakers exist in his generation. Obviously, he is better than Brett Ratner, or Micheal Bay, but compared to the generation before of Kubrick, Sydney Pollack and Roman Polanki (I realize he is from Poland) Anderson has a lot to live up to if he wants to be on the level of the big boys. That being said, Darjeerling Limited is a precious film which in theory is a great concept. Three emotionally damaged brothers from the leisure class exploring a land rife with spirituality, on a train together forced to cohabitat in close quarters while seeking "bonding" and "spiritual enlightenment", however, something is inherently contrived from the beginning. The opening short is somewhat pretentious in its attempt to become mysterious. Not without its charm, the hotel room is great, with its yellow and red interiors and Natalie Portman is gorgeous with short hair and grey overcoat, Wes Andersons true calling may be interior design because he dresses such pretty sets, but anyway, the film is clever without being laugh out loud funny and interesting for those like myself who have never been to India. The film does have its charm in that we have three lost souls drifting in a sea of loneliness all cramped together while speeding to some unknown and far off location. The problem is, we have seen Anderson do this before. I understand he is at the point now that he can pick and choose and continue to remake the same film, if he really wants to become a Kubrick, Polanski or Bunuel, he must make something different and show that he is more than a talented interior designer with a Beck like knowledge of film and the ability to cut and paste in order to build elaborate collages. Lets see him do a Horror film for starters, what made Kubrick great is that he could do comedy, horror, drama, period pieces, he was constantly pushing the envelope like any true artist yearns to do.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-10-27</date>
		<username>Maggie</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>I like it - so will you if you're like the characters</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Comedy is not my favorite category of movies but I really enjoyed seeing the Darjeeling Limited (DL) and highly recommend it, not just for the story, but also for its cinematography and soundtrack. While watching it, I was reminded of Dedication, a delightful romance/comedy film that I had seen about two months ago. If you like either or both of them, it is very likely that you do have something in common with those characters. Otherwise, you might have found them clueless and a waste of time.In DL, Francis, Peter and Jack are brothers who are trying to re-establish their bond with each other, while in Dedication, Henry is a cynical guy with obsessive-compulsive disorder finding himself in love with a warm-hearted girl. (I really can't help linking the two movies together although DL is way more sophisticated and subtle.) These characters are alike in the sense that they all have troubles in relating to other people, especially those they love, and sometimes they even cannot connect themselves with their own feelings. Somehow they have managed to get around their emotional weaknesses until a crisis happened that cast them into a situation requiring them to deal with their issues. In DL, Francis almost died in an accident, which pushed him to coordinate a journey to India to look for his mom with his brothers. It turns out that each of his brothers is having a difficult time with their loved ones. In Dedication, Henry's best friend died and he was left alone and lonely. Needless to say, the process of re-orienting themselves is not easy. This theme is actually a serious one while the humor of the movies is mostly manifested by the contradiction/struggle between what the characters say/do and what they actually think/ feel.I read some DL reviews, which have dismissed the characters as self-absorbed people. I do not agree and I feel empathetic with the characters instead. I see them as people who yearn for love but only find themselves very much disconnected. The mishap seems to have resulted from them unwittingly transferring their feelings towards family to their romantic love relationship. I was touched at different points at the movie, like Peter hiding himself in the toilet to cry over the short story written by Jack, who denies the fact that the story is actually about their family; Peter saying that he loves his wife but does not think their marriage will last long because the way he was brought up; Jack being obsessed by his ex-girlfriend after breaking up with her; the three brothers confronting their mom to find out the reason why their mom abandoned them when they needed her, etc. Another interesting character who is just as lost as the brothers is the Indian stewardess, who has to betray his boyfriend by fooling around with Jack before she allows herself the break-up. It is not clear what she feels about Jack but she was crying when Jack, along with his brothers, was kicked out of the train. She asked Jack," What's wrong with you?" and the answer given by Jack kind of saddened me ? " I don't know." Maybe because I am projecting myself to the characters or maybe it is the way that the director has meant to portray the characters, I feel that the brothers, especially Peter and Jack, are withholding their feelings in order to protect themselves. That is why Peter is expecting the failure of marriage and Jack would rather steal an affair with the stewardess than try to work things out with his girlfriend. (Note: I saw DL about a month ago and did not see Hotel Chevalier, which could have helped me understand more about what is going on between Jack and his girlfriend.) True, Jack did not know what was wrong with him and neither did his brothers - until after the funeral of the drowned boy whom they tried to save. Before the invitation of the funeral, they were heading home; after the funeral, they decided to face their fear of being rejected by their mom and go seek her out. What did the funeral do to them? It is an experience of reciprocal empathy.Given their "self-absorbed" personality, they would not have shown empathy to others if not for the life-and-death situation of encountering three drowning boys in an Indian local village. Their empathy was appreciated by the village even though one of the boys could not be saved. They were treated like part of the family of the drowned boy. I believe it is this experience of regaining empathy (that was displaced in their own family due to the run-away of the mom) that transformed the brothers. That is why although mom turned away from them again eventually, they did not grief and even become tougher than before. There is a scene where Francis was removing the bandage in a restroom. This act is a symbol of the brothers' reviewing their emotional wounds, which however still need time to heal. Nevertheless, now at least they are aware of what is wrong with them and what they want to do about it Just a few more words about Dedication ? the story is more straight forward. Henry was transformed by the empathetic responses from Lucy, the new love he found. And, the thoughts of Henry are mostly delivered to the audience by the words from his deceased friend in Henry's dream. In other words, the structure of the movie is simple compared with DL. Yet, it is very much worth seeing because of the wonderful acting of Billy Crudup.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>From a dull page, to a vibrant camera</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Wes Anderson is one of a handful of young American filmmakers who has amassed quite an impressive underground following after only a few films. Those that already know to expect offbeat, awkwardly written, eccentric characters ruling the screen will likely have a smile plastered across their face the entire duration of what is surely one of the more original road trip films to emerge in many years. Now, I do not belong to that Anderson cult. I've mildly enjoyed what little I've seen of his, but usually found him to be somewhat overrated when hearing him discussed in (younger) film circles.It does seem apparent that The Darjeeling Limited suffers from some of the same creative indulgences that stamped some of the substance out of his previous writing partnerships. Here even more so, we have a carefree, plot-light script which relishes in it's ambiguity almost as much as it's Western spiritual ironies. The trio of actors who carry the film provide an enjoyable, intuitive comedic chemistry between them, though rarely impress individually, even coming across as underdeveloped or lazy at times.Yet somehow for all it's shallow shortcomings, Darjeeling remained a transporting, visually ravishing, magical quirk-fest thanks to the wonderful direction of these three actors physically in individual shots, amazing locations, and inspired prop design. The attention to detail inside the train alone (where a good half the film takes place)leaps off the screen and sucks viewers into the half-baked odyssey. Because of Anderson's keen eye, even an unfulfilled conclusion does not really tarnish viewer's intimate and alternative trek through India as it has never been experienced before.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The movie is itself allegorical, right? The journey, the events could actually happen anywhere, not just on a trainride in a strange land? And so we see lives that have gone through various trauma, some of which is shared and some of which is idiosyncratic, and those events are revealed in different -metaphoric- ways. All events have an interconnectedness in this paradigm . . . nothing happens without some effect on everyone else, yet the effects are at times ignored or denied. Some of the effects show up dysfunctionally, some irrrationally, and some are hinted without being shown. The deeper ties that bind us are depicted as uncertain, impermanent or somehow unattainable to knowledge or experience. Do I have this right? I was not that taken with the movie.I usually like Wes Anderson's work, and wanted to like this one, but it left me a little cold. I wanted more and the more didn't materialize. I wanted it to go to a deeper level, and it couldn't or wouldn't. Maybe that was the point of the movie, and it succeeded....? Maybe I should watch it again. Maybe I have misjudged it. 		<fullReview>I wanted to like this movie much more than I did since I've liked many Wes Anderson movies in the past. The fact is that I didn't actually feel like the characters were in any way real. They were all exaggerated cartoons. Thanks for showing us your stylistic twitches.As profound as this movie wants to be, it often came off as simply pretentious and too clever by half.I'm sure the people will fall all over themselves to defend this movie, say it's the best thing ever made and they're happy that they're elite enough to understand it while anyone who criticizes it didn't truly understand.Fine, reinforce your clique if it makes you feel better.But perhaps I (and others who criticize it) understood it just fine, but didn't have a need to try reading in as much profoundness, where there really wasn't much of actual substance there. Lots of style, lots of repeated elements, lots of flash. No one would claim that it's lacking in style, just that it lacks in believability and real feeling. The cartoon characters didn't tug at my heart strings, sorry.It's probably worth seeing for its stylistic elements, I just wouldn't go in counting on lots more than that.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2007-10-27</date>
		<username>DonFishies</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>Hilarious, heartfelt, beautifully moving and just as offbeat as could be expected</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Despite the underlying pretentious nature of his films, I find that there is something to love or even downright adore in every Wes Anderson film. From Bottle Rocket all the way to The Life Aquatic with Steve Zissou, Anderson has managed to amalgamate dry wit and genuine human emotion into some of Hollywood's (and the independent film scene's) most personal films of the last two decades. And The Darjeeling Limited is no different, but can easily be said to be his best work since Rushmore.Shortly after their father's death, three brothers, Peter (Adrien Brody), Jack (Jason Schwartzman) and Francis (Owen Wilson), find themselves on a train in India. Francis, having organized the trip, wants the brothers to reconnect with one another, while traveling to some of the most holy locations in the country. Except they want to explore more than just holy lands.Despite being hyped to see another Anderson film, I have to say I was a little hesitant about the entire movie being set in India. It just gave off a bizarre vibe for me, and made me half assume that he went totally pretentious and was just going to deliver something akin to a Bollywood movie, but in his trademark style. But thankfully, it feels very much like the American film that it should be, and feels like it could easily meld itself within Anderson's filmography.The writing, as always, is top notch. While the actions and dialogue seemed to me to be a lot more slapstick than the usual not so subtle sand dry humour of his earlier films, Anderson manages to make it work. The words are so punctuated with that razor thin humour we are accustomed to, and the characters still act like they usually do. It just feels a whole lot less muted, and dare I say it, a little more mainstream. It does not have any adverse effects on the film at all, but it is worth noting especially for die-hard fans. The idiosyncrasies of the characters, while not amped to the point of overkill like in The Life Aquatic, are all very respectively done as well. They bring a lot of the biggest laughs for the film, but they also help hark back to the days of Rushmore and The Royal Tenenbaums. I am glad he continues to use them.What also works brilliantly for the film is the cinematography. Besides the classic shots and sequences typical to an Anderson movie (including some wondrous slow motion and plenty of tracking and lingering long shots), there are also many breathtaking shots of India. Whether out in the country or in the middle of a village, the film captures an enormous amount of colour and awe striking beauty. While I never noticed much care in the actual backgrounds until The Life Aquatic, Anderson seems very keen here on acquiring more than he needs in footage of India and its peoples. It gets overwhelming in some places, but only because it is so tastefully and meticulously created for the screen. It was definitely one of the highest points of the film.And of course, no Anderson film would be without its cast. The three main characters, played by Brody, Schwartzman and Wilson, are supposed to be neurotic and viciously confused brothers, and all three actors more than bring that sentiment to life. There is not one instant where they are not believable as the mismatched trio, and whether going for genuine laughs or genuine emotion, none of them drop the ball. They are very well tuned and primed for the idiosyncratic nature of their characters (Schwartzman actually helped write the script), and as quickly as the movie starts, they all disappear right into their performances. I think Brody does the best work here, but only because he is the most dead pan of the trio. It was nice to see some new blood being used by Anderson, and I hope his films only help bring him more talent.But on the same token, it was also a very nostalgic touch to include small (and tiny cameos) roles for quite a few members of his earlier films' casts from Bill Murray right down to Mr. Littlejeans himself, Kumar Pallana. Many of them do not offer anything truly significant to the film, but they help add to the thorough details in the backgrounds and foregrounds of scenes.If there is anything at all wrong with the film, it is the pacing. The film starts up quick, and blasts right through the introductions to the three brothers, before proceeding head first into their journey. But as the motivations and further elements of the journey begin panning into focus, the film loses its blinding speed and screeches to a halt. It picks up from time to time, but for a ninety minute film, it does drag itself a little. It does not get to the point of near boredom like in Life Aquatic, but it does take away ever so slightly from the film's lasting impact. Thankfully the story and acting are above and beyond excellent, so it helps the film not feel like it is stalling for no reason. But on the other hand, I just think they could have done without a few scenes, significant or not.I had my doubts, but Anderson and his crew helped prove me wrong on all counts. The Darjeeling Limited is another classic to add to his already offbeat repertoire, and it truly is the best one since Rushmore. He has helped construct a beautiful story about the search for identity, and in his own near-pretentious way, continued to show how easy it is for him to walk the line of dry humour and genuine human emotion. I loved this movie, and I wish every movie could leave me smiling like this one did.9.5/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-12-02</date>
		<username>castipiani</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Still in his groove, but . . .</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A number of earlier commenters say they feel viewers have to take Anderson's films, technique, values, and all, as they come: He's found his groove, enjoy it or not. For me, Darjeeling Limited seems to show a filmmaker struggling to get OUT of his groove, while not abandoning the comforts of home.Among the latter: the utter irrelevance of money to his main characters; to call them "privileged" is wildly understated. They are so free of money worries that it becomes one of the main burdens they lash out against, without ever giving up one iota of the benefits offered. And not just money: Jack (co-writer Jason Schwartzman) is a "writer" whose Paris pied-a-terre is a luxury hotel suite, where he's supposed to be hiding out from "an ex-girl friend" (Natalie Portman, for God's sake: How privileged can you GET?) who flies in just to bed him.As always, Anderson's theme is finding meaning in a world where there are no limits to one's will, where you can only distinguish yourself from your velvet-plush background by wearing too much eyeshadow or running around the plains of India barefoot in a Briony suit. But unlike other Anderson films I've seen, here not only the characters but the film itself seems to be hurling itself (decorously, of course) against the walls of the doll-house. There's something scary about the joyful way the brothers throw their travel plans aside when they're invited to attend the funeral of the Indian child they failed to save from drowning: Whoopee, they seem to say, a chance at last to encounter Real Life, maybe we'll finally FEEL something! (This is not a criticism of Anderson or his film; I admire the cold honesty required to let this scene happen.) India, with all its turmoil and color, doesn't let film or filmmaker out of the doll-house. There's a truly eerie (studio) tracking shot late in the film in which most of the cast are seen, each in their own little candy-box train compartment, while the real Indian countryside scrolls by outside the windows. Anderson's not outside the box yet, but his awareness of the box is more than ever what his film is about.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>India inked</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Following a troika of greats with "Rushmore", "The Royal Tenenbaums" and "The Life Aquatic with Steve Zissou", Wes Anderson extended it with "The Darjeeling Limited". This one has brothers Peter (Adrien Brody), Jack (Jason Schwartzman) and Francis (Owen Wilson) traveling together on a train through India for a "spiritual journey". The dysfunctional family is clearly Wes Anderson's specialty, and he doesn't disappoint. Especially impressive is the scene showing the brothers before the trip to India (I liked how the scene throws the audience off).Are dysfunctional families becoming a cliché in Anderson's movies? Well, suspense was a cliché in Alfred Hitchcock's films, and Hitch made great use of it. Wes Anderson has made another good film here, and I recommend it. Part of the point is that, despite these people's problems, they're well-meaning. I can see the brothers as a branch of the brood in "The Royal Tenenbaums", with the luggage representing the emotional baggage with which the father left them (and then what they do to the luggage at the end).Anyway, a really good one. Anderson so far hasn't made a bad movie, and so I hope that he doesn't disappoint with "Fantastic Mr. Fox" (currently in production). Also starring Waris Ahluwalia, Amara Karan, Barbet Schroeder, Wallace Wolodarsky, Anjelica Huston, Natalie Portman, Irfan Khan and Bill Murray.</fullReview>

		<username>jzappa</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A Guilelessly Vague Work of Material Beauty</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This guilelessly vague dramatic comedy is a warm, engaging, funny film, very immersed in India, a land and culture of baffling beauty, and thus a brilliant opportunity for the stunning showcase of an art director. This Venusian journey of materialistic and tangible beauties begins with Bill Murray (I just contradicted myself, didn't I?) racing to catch a train in the Indian countryside. As he is about to lose the train we see Adrien Brody following the same desperate intention. Brody makes it, Murray doesn't. Brody makes his way to his compartment, where he finds his brothers, played by Jason Schwartzman and Owen Wilson. Wilson's head is heftily bandaged, as he was in a serious motorcycle crash. Brody and Schwartzman have come to India, apparently at Wilson's proposal, on the Darjeeling Limited, on what he wishes to be some kind of spiritual excursion.This is a great beginning which demonstrates the very character sustained throughout the film. Wilson plays an obsessive sort, which is why his younger brothers find it grueling to be in his company, which tugs the easygoing movie along. One of the film's draws is its Indian framework. Wes Anderson and his co-writers Jason Schwartzman and Roman Coppola avoid clear inducements of the exotic by surprising us. The stewardess on the train, for example, speaks standard English and seems American.Anderson employs India thankfully not as if he and his audience are tourists, but as a surrounding that is extremely palpable, a testament to the true power of cinema without the vain aide of Blu-Ray and Hi-Def revolutionizing, as we can practically smell India and dread the ferocious sun and the feeling of sweat. Take into account a long-lasting scene in which Wilson, Brody and Schwartzman sit at a table in the diner car with an Indian stranger with whom they do not trade a word. He keeps to himself. The brothers confront each other directly and overtly about their points of conflict and reservation. The Indian does not react in any apparent way. Then again, nobody ever does in a Wes Anderson film. But his tranquil being there is a camouflaged concentration of scene nicety, because all four of them could probably converse in English very well. But I think the world learns a great deal from Americans when we travel and assume that foreigners are not fluent in English. You think you like people-watching, it's people across the globe such as this Indian who take it to a whole new level.I also like how Wilson, Schwartzman and Brody elbow one another in an intimate, familial way. Yes, the film is competent with great presence and simple being and possession of itself, but it does wander. But it doesn't have an ultimate objective, other than perhaps a visceral experience of existence. Anderson may not have used a straight, constantly progressing story structure before either, but he clearly knows it, because the humor and poignancy come in the way he meddles with non-sequiturs and inconvenient interruptions of rambling.Note: Watch Hotel Chevalier with The Darjeeling Limited. Hotel Chevalier is the short film that precedes this story. It is a fun way of feeling more deeply in the know with some characters and more informed than other characters. It also has that Venusian subtlety of touch that Wes Anderson seems to bring to everything.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Not perfect, but perfect in that it isn't.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Darjeeling Limited is a spectacular movie. It is directed by Wes Anderson, the king of quirk. Though everything that happens in it is pretty ridiculous, as all his movies are, somehow it seems like that's the truest reality, like no other actions could be taking place. Nothing else would make sense.The Darjeeling Limited is about three brothers who take a spiritual journey to India to become better brothers. Francis (Owen Wilson), spurred by his survival in a motorcycle crash, calls the journey together on the train. Peter (Adrien Brody) has left his very pregnant wife to go on this journey. He is not happy about the kid. He always figured they'd divorce. And not because he doesn't love her, he just always expected it. Jack (Jason Schwartzman) hasn't been in the United States in a long time, and has been living in hotels, trying to get over his girlfriend, or get with her, or do something in his post-break up period of life."Hotel Chevalier" is a short film (to be shown before the movie). It tells the story of Jack and his girlfriend (Natalie Portman). He had been hiding from her in a hotel in Paris, but she comes and visits him.Separating these parts into two separate ones is a brilliant decision. There is no way "Hotel Chevalier" could be put into The Darjeeling Limited, and The Darjeeling Limited can be seen without "Hotel Chevalier," but it completes it and makes it into one continuous and brilliant piece of art.It is Wes Anderson's most Wes Anderson-y movie to date, but I also think it's one of his more accessible films, disregarding Rushmore. There isn't as much camp as most of his movies, and it's not as funny throughout as most of his movies, but it still has that amazing Wes Anderson light gravity. I think the multicultural-ness of the whole thing is a positive; Anderson writes and directs it spectacularly, with amazing care. It's almost like a precursor to Slumdog Millionaire, the American's first steps into India without getting fulling wet, a fully American movie that just happens in India, thus transforming it into an Indian movie.The screenplay was written by Wes Anderson, Roman Coppola, and Jason Schwartzman, Anderson's best screenplay-writing group to date. Schwartzman and Wilson are no outsiders to Anderson's movies and therefore do wonderfully, as they truly understand their roles. Brody is a new addition, but does spectacularly, and in my opinion is the best in the bunch. Anjelica Huston is the mother-goddess in this movie as well, a force that drives almost every Anderson film. No one lives without her permission, and they can only live according to her standards. It's such a fascinating character that is present in The Royal Tenenbaums, The Life Aquatic with Steve Zissou, and The Darjeeling Limited. The art direction in this movie is brilliant like all of his others. He understands an aspect ratio like no other director I've seen. He knows how to use a full screen, and make the space a character itself in the movie.It's nothing short of wonderful, true, poignant, brilliant, ridiculous, and stunning. No character has an unmotivated action, even though it seems they all are acting however they want whenever they want. You can tell exactly why they all do what they do. It's just so perfect. Not perfect, but perfect in that it isn't.</fullReview>

		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>If it isn't broke, why fix it?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The films of Wes Anderson have been compared to the cartoons that appear in 'The New Yorker' and the cartoons that appear in 'The New Yorker' are not like the cartoons that appear anywhere else. It's as if they were designed, not just with 'The New Yorker' in mind, but so that they would be integral to that magazine and just couldn't exist outside of it and you either catch the joke or you don't and even if you catch the joke you could be forgiven for thinking that maybe they aren't that funny. Clever? Very. Funny? Well, funny peculiar rather than funny ha-ha. And each successive film of Wes Anderson is becoming more and more like a series of 'New Yorker' cartoons animated into something approximating to real life. Not that you are ever likely to meet these people, and if you did meet them you might find yourself driven to contemplate suicide or murder. No-one in a Wes Anderson film goes out of their way to ingratiate themselves to you."The Darjeeling Limited" is no exception. It's a true original and yet it is utterly familiar. That's right, we met these guys before in "The "The Life Aquatic with Steve Zissou" and before that in "The Royal Tenenbaums"; not precisely the same guys but everyone in a Wes Anderson film is drawn with the same brush, they all look the same and they all speak with the same voice. If you don't like one Wes Anderson movie you won't like any Wes Anderson movie. Fortunately, I love Wes Anderson's movies and again, "The Darjeeling Limited" is no exception. It is just as gorgeous to look at, (indeed, more so), just as funny, (and yes, I do laugh at Wes Anderson's films), and just as touching, (thematically these are movies about family and love and affection), as his earlier works.If there is a common theme running through Wes Anderson's films it is family and the relationship between siblings and their parents. Anderson may milk such relationships for their comic potential but neither is he afraid to introduce a totally discordant note either. It isn't all smart gags; people die as well, (here it's a child). Consequently, "The Darjeeling Limited" is one of the most moving of Anderson's films, (which doesn't mean it isn't very funny almost at the same time as it is drawing our tears).Despite it's exotic location, (this time three brothers make a 'spiritual' journey across India), it's probably closer in tone to "The Royal Tenenbaums" than it is to "The Life Aquatic ..." and they would make a perfect double-bill. Some critics have bemoaned Anderson's apparent 'failure' to do something different even when moving half way across the world but if it isn't broke, why fix it? No-one else could have made this movie; in that respect it's unique. It is also some kind of classic and I can't wait to see it again.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Yes, I am a fan...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>and I find myself wishing more and more after every movie Wes makes that he was actually a friend of mine in real life. I like his artistic and unique point of view as a director and I think he's got a great sense of humor. I believe the trouble some people have with his movies comes from a set of expectations we all have going into a movie we've never seen before. It comes from that commercial formula most movies fit which is harmless so long as it's backed by good writing, directing, and acting---PLUS---those tricky-ass movie trailers we see, so rarely do they squash down a whole story into a real snippet of what you're going to pay for. I find that especially true about all of Wes Anderson's movies. They're so very different and unconventional that the marketing people CLEARLY don't know what the heck to do: his movies don't squash well. I LUV Royal Tenenbaums, I think it's my absolute favorite Wes movie but going back and watching the previews that were made for it, they don't do diddley squat in getting it right. SO the thing I've found with this movie as with The Life Aquatic is that it was 1000 times more fun the second go around. I remember seeing The Life Aquatic in the theater and wanting so badly to love it from the get go like I did Tenenbaums and instead found myself totally unnerved and unable to sit back and enjoy the story because it was so loose and, while entertaining, unsettling. I was apprehensive the whole way through about what might happen next in the story. Then I watched it on DVD and it was like a WHOLE OTHER MOVIE---and I LUVED IT. I still crack up at every great, wry Bill Murray line it.So, after watching the Darjeeling Limited for the first time and feeling unsure of it altogether, I decided to leave my initial reactions as they were, wait a few weeks and then watch it again to see what strung itself together for me upon a second viewing. After the second viewing: Bingo! Loved it. I found myself smiling, laughing, truly observing and noticing all kinds of soft little nuances as they came into fullness for me. It all came through: the acting, the look and feel of it, and the story. Of course, it has moments in it that will be a lot funnier to you if you come from a bigger family. I'm one of four girls and the oldest so I absolutely LOVED the part in the temple where Owen Wilson's character looks down at Adrien Brody while all three brothers are kneeling to pray and says "Is that my belt?" and Adrien Brody (the middle brother) says, "Oh, yeah. Can I borrow it?" then Owen answers, "No, not right now. I was going to wear that, I was looking all over for it this morning. Give it back." Adrien Brody rolls his eyes and relinquishes the belt, Owen follows the handing over of said belt with, "Next time, ask first." I JUST ABOUT DIED----THAT---THAT little scene RIGHT THERE sums up the biggest, longest living gripe that I have as the oldest sibling---the younger ones always take your stuff!!! ALWAYS!!! always---every oldest sibling will tell you that is the gospel truth, my friend. I have said, "Next time ask first" and "I was looking all over for that, " or, "I was going to wear that" like a broken record for the better half of my life! I also loved how Owen's character was the list maker, the one who felt he had to be in charge and take care of his brothers (which they totally resented) and yet at the same time could act so selfishly. I do that myself to greater or lesser degrees. It got me thinking that the selfishness is a natural reaction to feeling like you have to be responsible for your younger siblings all the time or you used to always have to be. It's sort of like being made into an extra parent without being asked,---your mom and dad let you know all the time that you have to watch so-and-so, and you're in charge while they're gone and it's your ass if they do anything stupid, blah, blah, you're the oldest, you're the oldest, you're the oldest... and you love your siblings, you do---but the kid in you that just wants to be a kid and not responsible for anybody misses that luxury dearly so you act selfishly sometimes to just be you and not "the responsible one", even as an adult.I just want to say: I can COMPLETELY understand why there are Wes Haters I do. I get where the haters are coming from. It's a tricky thing, being made to "work" to find what the director is trying to do with a movie. If you don't get it, it was a failure, if you see it, what a clever, artful success. It's okay if you don't like it, don't get it. I'm sad 'cause I feel like you're missing out, but that's okay. Different strokes for different folks. Just don't be so mean to my pal Wes, he doesn't make movies to tick you off---he makes them for himself, he makes them to tell stories, and he makes them for everybody else out there like me that he's won over by doing so in a way we just can't help but freakin' love---and us Wes Fans, we freakin' love the way he just keeps on doing his very own thing, disregarding any negative feedback. That's the best part, he does the work HE wants to do without compromising it for a minute. C'mon---as a hater you at the very least have to appreciate that kind of selfish, artistic integrity---the man doesn't sell out. Pretty rare in Hollywood, I think.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>It figures that the first Wes Anderson film critics and audiences decided to turn on would be the one where I found myself liking him again. I despised The Royal Tenenbaums and thought The Life Aquatic was a slight improvement but still disappointing. I can say what I don't like about Tenenbaums and Aquatic, and I think I know the difference, but, not having seen either of those two films since they came out, it's mere speculation. Perhaps if I went back and rewatched them, I would find that my tastes have changed enough for me to allow them into my heart. For the first time ever, that's tempting. My perception now of the difference is this: I found the dialogue and plots of Tenenbaums and Aquatic to lumber around lackadaisically. I found myself paying way more attention to the vibrant visuals and great music on the soundtrack (both certainly on display here; that song that plays during the accompanying short Hotel Chevalier, and also briefly in Darjeeling, is freaking awesome), trying to avoid being too annoyed (worked the second time around, but I was merely annoyed at Tenenbaums). I never felt Anderson convinced me of the universe he was creating with these two films. The actors really didn't seemed convinced to me, either. The Darjeeling Limited seems like it has more of a purpose, like it moves forward without too much dilly-dallying. It's fast paced, and the stylized dialogue convinced me of the film's universe. In the end, I don't think The Darjeeling Limited is much more than a lark, but it's so much fun that I didn't feel like I had wasted my time. I laughed, and a lot, where I didn't at all during the previous two films. And I did find it touching at certain points. Maybe I could latch onto it because I am one of three brothers myself. Overall, a marvelous movie</fullReview>
