		<username>richard_sleboe</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Monogram canvas</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Words go a long way, but they cannot do justice to this quadruple masterpiece that pleases eyes, ears, mind and soul alike. Wes Anderson takes us on a spiritual journey to India and creates a cosmopolitan fairy tale kingdom all his own in the process. Watched over by Louis Vuitton and Ray Davies as their unseen patron saints, the brothers Whitman go through the laughter and the tears (and the substance abuse) of a forced family reunion, haunted by the ghosts of distant parents and absent lovers. As part of Andersons's web of self-referential puns, Owen Wilson's control freak character Francis is created in the image of Chas Tenenbaum, the paranoid fire-drilling father of twins played by Ben Stiller in "The Royal Tenenbaums". On another level, the way Francis plans and handles the brothers' journey is an allegory of Wes Anderson's meticulous attention to detail as a director. The spring-steel train ticket holders, Peter's tinted Yuri Andropov glasses and the Hotel Chevalier "guest in residence" stationery Jack uses for his short story are among my personal favorites. Lovingly designed, spectacularly lit and superbly acted, "The Darjeeling Limited" is built to last. Even before the closing credits started rolling, I wanted to see it all over again, and I will. Guest appearance by Bill Murray in the part of a business traveler in such a hurry he must have the devil himself on his heels.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-10-28</date>
		<username>Howard Schumann</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Lacking in genuine emotion</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Co-written by Roman Coppola and Jason Schwartzman, Wes Anderson's The Darjeeling Limited is, like many of his films, a deadpan comedy about family dysfunction that flirts with emotional depth but never quite reaches it. This one, however, has an exotic setting in India, the enchanting music of Satyajit Ray, gorgeous photography of some of India's holy sites, and a lot of talk about a "spiritual journey". Yet this tale of the travels of bored young people of exaggerated wealth makes me wonder what spiritual growth means to Anderson other than as something to make people feel better about their unproductive lives. The film may be eventually be considered a sequel to The Royal Tennenbaums, a film in which children are adversely affected by the absence of their father, or it could also be looked at as another remake of Night of the Living Dead.This time it looks as if the three unhappy children are attempting to move to a different level, but it would not be cool if they actually discovered something larger than themselves to believe in. The three are brothers Francis (Owen Wilson), Jack (Jason Schwartzman), and Peter Whitman (Adrian Brody) who have as much resemblance to each other as Fay Wray and King Kong. The only thing they seem to have in common is that they are all obscenely wealthy, sporting 3,000 dollar loafers, eleven-piece designer suitcases by Louis Vuitton, and other accouterments of privilege that they bring without any reluctance to one of the poorest countries in the world. They are also loaded down with pain pills, cough syrup, and muscle relaxants and seem to be functioning on a somewhat primitive emotional level.Francis, a control freak who organizes every moment to the max, is all banged up from a motorcycle accident and bandages cover his face. Peter is unhappily married, talks about divorce, yet his wife Alice is expecting a boy in the next few months, so naturally the place he wants to be is 3,000 miles away. Jack, a short-story writer, has a thing about checking his ex-wife's answering machine and going barefoot for no obvious reason. After outrunning a tired businessman (Bill Murray), they board the long-distance train mocked up for the film called "The Darjeeling Limited". Francis' goal is to travel through India, see the holy sites, and meet up with their mother (Angelica Huston), a nun who teaches poor children in a remote convent in the Himalayas. The three haven't spoken to each other for over a year since their father's funeral but are reunited at the instigation of Francis who tells them that "we are here to find each other".Before long, however, they are squabbling as tensions grow about who their father loved more, what secrets they have kept from each other, and who is entitled to their father's belt and other belongings. Though on one level, the film can be looked on as a satire of the ugly American surrounded by beauty but unable to locate it on the map, the characters are cartoonish and never come to life, either as obnoxious tourists or spiritual pretenders. When the film takes on an aspect of reality in a very affecting sequence involving the drowning of a young boy, it seems incongruous, as if the projectionist spliced it in from another movie, and Anderson uses this tragedy only as a foil for the brothers to recall their father's funeral and discover their need to complete their journey.There is some hanky panky aboard the train as Jack pursues the stewardess Rita (Amara Karan), a caricature of the exotic dark-skinned beauty, but it seems inappropriate and empty of feeling. The boys in fact act out to the extent they are thrown off the train when they bring a poisonous snake on board and get into a fight in which Jack sprays mace on his brothers. Anderson has so much talent and had me thinking several times that the film was going someplace special and it does seem as if the brothers do come away with something valuable for themselves - quite by accident. Yet the final part of the trip, however, which is supposed to be the focal point of the film, turns out to be more of an afterthought and The Darjeeling Limited, in spite of its stunning color landscapes and hints of deeper feelings, winds down to a place lacking in genuine emotion.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Please drop your expectations and enjoy a good film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Wes Anderson has, for better or worse, become a respected and recognizable figure in film. This is great for those who truly appreciate his work, and bad for those who expect him to merely continue to be his previous work.This film is not perfect. I would not by any stretch say that it is his weakest film, but, despite obvious visual, personal and musical cues, it is his most different.The characters (and actors) certainly seem familiar. The look is 100% true Wes Anderson. The general feel is there and, yet, it seems to be a different take on his normal cast of sheepishly melancholic comedy characters. Things are much more personal this time.No, the movie is not attempting at anything profound (and, perhaps because it is less of an out-and-out comedy people are looking for profundity {and, perhaps, his ardent supporters are, sometimes, a little too ardent}), but it delivers in a familiar, happily-sad way which can be very effective when one is truly open to its openness.The worst thing one can do when going into this movie is want it to be something else. It should be taken on its own terms, accepted on its own terms, and understood as the modest, sweet, humorous tale that it is. It won't make any top 10 lists right away, but maybe its genuine simplicity and inexplicability will, in time, make it all the more memorable. It might just be the movie Wes Anderson has, truly, always wanted to make, and that should really be appreciated, for what it is.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-11-29</date>
		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Likable and bizarre comedy that contains a couple of undertones that I'm not sure the director wanted to be there.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When I watch films like these, I always look at the protagonists who are going on a journey of self discovery at the very beginning and then compare them at the very end to what they were like at the beginning; just to see if the script was smart enough to develop them in a sophisticated and entertaining way ? with The Darjeeling Limited, I did not get that satisfaction that I got out of films such as Thelma &amp; Louise and Road to Perdition. The film is, at its core, a road movie as was Thelma &amp; Louise and Road to Perdition although it's a single line journey that doesn't take place on a road but a train line for the best part ? it does venture off the railway line for certain reasons and the film lagged because of it but that's a separate issue. Where I saw this film, we were shown Anderson's short Hotel Chevalier about one of the feature film's main characters called Jack (Schwartzman) and his one night stand with an ex-girlfriend in Paris. I'm sure everyone enjoyed it for the almost naked Natalie Portman but remove her nudity from the short and you don't have much else except for the good use of a certain music track played through Jack's iPod which aided in the effectiveness of the feature.So with the film sort of started before it had actually started, I was intrigued at where The Darjeeling Limited was going to go. Unfortunately, the film does not hold up in the long run. I understood the journey of enlightenment and self discovery, something a lot of films tackle (or used to) and what better place to deal with such a subject than India what with all its spiritual beliefs and religion's that rely on meditation. There is definitely an undertone or an undercurrent running parallel with the main, routine narrative of three brothers on a journey but I couldn't figure out exactly what it was since there was so much bizarre content towards the end involving seemingly pointless and random stuff that I was zoning out. On the surface and for what it is, The Darjeeling Limited is a funny at times and entertaining tale about three brothers on a journey: the opening forty to fifty minutes are great fun with snappy dialogue, funny situations and good pacing throughout but oddly enough, as soon as they leave the train for certain reasons the film looses all the punch and energy and becomes something a little slower and a little different; suddenly, it looses its humour and it looses its focus since it's not a comedy anymore but some sort of emotional drama. The scene that sums this up is upon leaving the train, the brothers are at loggerheads and yet a certain event involving a child in peril forces them to re-join just when required.I also question director Wes Anderson's commitment to his content. The film already changes genre; which although isn't a bad thing if done correctly, but here it's for the worst. But the deeper problems lie in his consistency: the Indians on the train are poorly portrayed since the guard is a liar and a thief when it comes to the snake and the Indian woman is far too loose to be committed to her religion: she is an Indian character written by an American with American female character traits in mind; thus she comes across as a bit of a slut. I also found the undertones of fascism and Nazism a little disturbing. Maybe I'm reading too much into it but what was the relevance of the German couple at the beginning? Furthermore, the name of the garage the brother's father left his car at is entitled 'Luftwaffe Motors' Luftwaffe is the name of the German air force; the same air force that the British went to war with in 1940 ? why does Anderson use the name 'Luftwaffe'? Thirdly, there are swastikas painted on walls at various points in the film: one is on the ticket office when they are thrown off the train the second is chalked onto a wall nearer the end. I know the religion of Jainism uses the Swastika as their emblem and did so centuries before the Nazi's but with so many German references up to this point; you have to wonder what the hell was going on and whether there was a connection.But this is the film summed up. It starts off like it is going to be a good film about an important trip at three points in three peoples various lives but plays out once it, quite literally, comes off the rails. I hated the way the camera would pan instead of cutting to the next shot (cute the first time Wes but not after a while); I didn't like the way lingering shots of the boy's funeral and Indian culture as a whole were lingered on since Anderson is basically trying to educate us on a subject I don't think even he knows much about: the relaxed attitude to being in India; the portrayal of the Indian train stewardess and the way Peter (Brody) is almost mimicking a spiritual Indian act towards the end by doing his own 'Indian' dance/pose as he lets the feather go ? was that supposed to make us laugh? If so, why would the director think it was funny and the other rituals (such as the boy's funeral procedures and the spots on the forehead) not funny. This is a film people will enjoy on the surface but look deeper and there is certainly something there that is unnerving. The music is fantastic, the acting and overall characterisation is good and Anderson does make good use of the setting visually if not culturally. There are moments in The Darjeeling Limited that are funny but not in a laugh out loud way but that's the consistency of the film: subtle.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Revolutionary movie that proves you don't need a big budget or flashy special effects to make a great Film!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To everyone that thinks all Wes Anderson movies are the same, The Darjeeling Limited begs to differ. With a bizarre plot, quirky characters, and absolutely amazing acting, Anderson's newest film does literally everything right to break from the mold, and it's certainly not your typical Hollywood film. Darjeeling is filled to the very end with subtle humor and never relies on nasty gags or stupid catch phrases. The film stars Adrien Brody, Jason Schwartzman, Owen Wilson, and Anjelica Houston, along with a couple cameos from Natalie Portman and Bill Murray.Three brothers who haven't spoken to each other in a year set off on a spiritual train voyage across India to discover themselves as people and become friends like they used to be. Francis (Owen Wilson) is the annoying, know-it-all control freak, Jack (Jason Schwartzman) is the quiet writer looking for romance and love, and Peter (Adrien Brody) is the outspoken middle child who is generally freaking out because he has a baby on the way. The three personalities of the characters mesh into one big, epic journey that scales all across the desert and India, most of the time on the train called The Darjeeling Limited.The acting and the dialogue is the best thing about this film, and the subtle humor makes for lots of laugh-out-loud moments. Owen Wilson gives his all to make his character Francis incredibly annoying and self-centered, wanting to control literally everything around him. Adrien Brody was my favorite actor out of the bunch, and it's so refreshing to see him appear in an indie-type film, as he usually sticks to the big-budget blockbusters like 2005's King Kong. He gives a really memorable performance and is a stand-out even among a very solid cast. Jason Schwartzman continues giving sensational performances in all of his movies, and this one is no exception. Ever since seeing him in Slackers, it was so obvious that this guy had a huge future ahead of him. Anjelica Houston, as always, absolutely shines in her small role. While she was disgustingly underused, her performance was one of the best in the film.Darjeeling Limited is yet another sensational Wes Anderson film with non-stop slow-motion musical montages and lovable characters. The best part of any Anderson movie is how the ending is happy, and yet there is always just a tinge of melancholy to it. This ending is no exception. While it cannot be said that Darjeeling is Anderson's best film to date, it is definitely one of the best movies of the year and an altogether amazing movie.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Three brothers and a "spiritual" quest</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Darjeeling Limited (2007) was co-written and directed by Wes Anderson. It's basically a picaresque film about three brothers who travel across India by train on a spiritual quest.As it turns out, the quest is more personal than spiritual--the men want to recapture the close relationship they had as children. The actors who play the three brothers are all highly competent professionals--Owen Wilson, Adrien Brody, and Jason Schwartzman. Wilson was brave enough to be filmed for almost the entire movie with his his face hidden by bandages. (Medical note: the bandages were as clean and pristine at the end of the movie as they were in the beginning. Absolutely impossible in view of the plot.) As in a picaresque novel, the brothers experience interesting, unusual, and--in one case--tragic situations on their journey. They also meet interesting people--the lovely Amara Karan plays a stewardess on the train, and Waris Ahluwalia plays the chief steward, who is not amused by the antics of the brothers. (For some reason, Anderson has chosen an actor for this role who grew up in Brooklyn, and has no trace of Indian-accented English.)Darjeeling Limited has real strengths--the incredible scenery, the fine acting, and a glimpse of a world that appears highly exotic to us. On the other hand, I didn't find any of the characters particularly likable, and I found the ending disappointing. It's certainly worth seeing the film for its positive qualities, but it's not worth a special detour. Incidentally, we saw the film in a theater. It will definitely lose something on a small screen.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Misanthropy as Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this movie yesterday, when it opened here, and generally disliked it. I confess that I am not a Wes Anderson supporter: if I liked Wes Anderson films, then I would probably have liked this film, since it does seem very like other Wes Anderson films. But I disliked it probably as much as other Wes Anderson films.What struck me most strongly this time was that I suddenly realized why Wes Anderson films ... or at least this one ... are so easy for me to dislike. It is because they seem above all imbued with a sort of consistent misanthropy.Or at least this one does. The stylistic and structural elements are not repugnant to me. The cinematography seemed to me to be stylized but delightful. The scenes generally unfold as highly stylized tableaux vivants only loosely concatenated by a very loose plot structure. But the humor was what was most striking. I felt like I was once again a sophomore in high school, with a sneer on my lips for anyone and anything around me. And that is the kind of view of life that I feel I am invited to in this movie. Everything, and everyone, deserves a sneer, seems to be the Anderson message. Even ... in a particularly brutal sequence ... the death of a child.Contrast another film maker known for his stylized approach to film: Quentin Tarantino. A Tarantino movie seems to invite me to almost the opposite of a sneer for everything; rather, I feel invited to like and respect and find something good and worthwhile in everything. In an Anderson movie, in this one in particular, I am invited to view everything with an amused contempt.Thanks but no thanks.I did love the scenes in India. The acting of the main characters seemed competently consistent with the tableaux vivants they adorned. There is a sort of crisp cadence to the unfolding of the loose concatenation of scenes that to me was appealing.But all in all, I think I'd rather watch things that don't invite me to that sophomoric sneer that I thought I had long ago left behind.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Rant_Bralding</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>a perhaps misunderstood example of film as metaphoric</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Like the Nightmare on Elm Street or the Nightmare Before Christmas, Citizen Cane or Hell in the Pacific, The Darjeeling Limited is another example of Hollywood's pseudo-Eastern process of meta-absorption. In other words, Wes Anderson's film, like many of his made by others before him, is a metaphor for another film. Audiences may be initially confused by this interpretation, as his previous films explored an opposite paradigm of group dynamics in which the story's multiplicity was self contained; however, there are plenty of clues in the film's narrative and presentation to provide an entry to understanding. The introduction of The Darjeeling Limited not through a preview, but through a short film, Hotel Darchevaling, should clue anybody into the frame of meta-referentiality. Natalie Portman's actual nudity, as opposed to publicity concerning nudity in another film (whose name escapes me), which did not happen, is a crude way to establish direct lines of meaning between different narratives; an obvious concession to the American consciousness circa 2008, but one which we can accept, as everything, after all is context. The film itself is quick to provide other clues to it's unusual but not unprecedented mode of discourse. Bill Murray falls away, the stalwart usual suspect of Anderson's social dynamics left at the wayside to signal a new paradigm. The camera-work is a wide and linear, simultaneously distancing the viewer and exposing them to more of the action; a surefire technique to place the psyche onto subconscious tracks suggesting referentiality rather than interdependence. Most tellingly, the film is not, as tradition dictates, concerned with action, but with dialogue about action. This is not, I have determined, a commentary on American politics and life, as other critics have suggested, but rather more simply a necessity dictated by the film's nature, its entire narrative floating sheetlike above that of another; the social commentary which this may suggest is merely an ontological side effect, meaning created through similar structures in the relationship of concept to reality substrate. If there is any meaning in the similarity of the film's structure to structures in the outside world, it is accidental, and therefore all the more powerful. But to return; a question has most likely by this point suggested itself to you: what is the actual film, if The Darjeeling Limited is the metaphor? You can see it in the final shot: as the characters run to catch the train, a solitary figure also runs in the field in the background, heading towards some unseen goal perhaps 300 feet to the right of the train's direction of motion. I do not know what this goal is - the train is in the way - and by consequence I cannot identify the film. But the mystery, as in all films, is both the battle and the love.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-01-16</date>
		<username>Harry T. Yung (harry_tk_yung@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Room for the audiences' interpretation</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>That "The Darjeeling Limited" is a movie about family dysfunction would suggest a logical continuation of director Wes Anderson's "The royal Tenenbaums". Whether it's a comedy or not is debatable. While there are absurdities aplenty, the caricature funniness of the three brothers is overshadowed by the always palpable sadness that possesses them.Set on a "spiritual journey" after their father's death, purportedly to rekindle some of their brotherhood bond, the three brothers are presented to the audience in three different levels of self-indulgence. Oldest Francis (Owen Wilson) is easily recognizable as one with an impulsive obsession to manage and control things (and other people), and towards the end, it is painfully obvious to us that this comes from his mother. In the middle is Peter (Adrien Brody) who seems always consumed in sulking anger and resigning gloominess, deteriorating relationship with his wife being perhaps the main reason. Inscrutable youngest brother Jack (Jason Schwartzman) seems completely devoid of any feeling until it comes to women, as exemplified by constantly tapping into his ex-girlfriend's (Natalie Portman) phone to hear voice messages, or losing no time in striking up a liaison with the train's attractive attendant Rita (Amara Karan).If this is a road movie (or a rail movie, to be exact), there is no particular destination. Oh yes, Peter reveals to his brothers in the middle of the journey that they are going to look for their mother (Angelic Huston), to find out why she failed to show up at her husband's funeral. Finding her however raised more questions than gave answers. It's as though what director Anderson wanted to do was to array a collection of raw emotions and let the audience assemble them as they please. Visually, there is always something interesting going on, as Anderson seems to have gone all out to make each frame a stimulating visual experience. The colour reminds me of Pedro Alomdover, not because these two directors' employment of colour scheme is similar, but just because they are each provoking in its own way.That this movie is an open invitation to the audience to interpret is perhaps best underscored by the character played by Bill Murray. It shouldn't even be called a character as this is only a cameo appearance as a businessman who fails to catch the train that Peter succeeds in hopping on to meet his brothers in the beginning. Some like to see him as the father who died, symbolically. Others have different interpretations. Take your pick.What really deserves mentioning is the 13-minute prologue "Hotel Chevalier" that was filmed as an entirely separate movie, complete with opening title and concluding credit roll. This is where Natalie Portman appears (except for a 2-second cameo in the main body of the movie) as the ex-girlfriend who tracks down and visits Jack in a hotel room in Paris where he is in some kind of self-imposed retreat. This short sketch is exquisitely filmed and in that sense deserves to be considered a separate piece of cinematic work.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Another Anderson Classic.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Wes Anderson returns with another subtle and beautiful comedy/drama about three brothers trying to come to terms with their father's death while taking a spiritual journey across India. Marring together traditional camera styles and that of bollywood films Anderson weaves the story through a tapestry of colourful set pieces and sibling rivalry. Brody, Wilson and Schwartzman play the brothers brilliantly each with their own unique mannerisms and ticks. Although different in some ways from his earlier films it still contains his trademark mixed bag soundtrack, use of slow motion and unrivalled attention to detail in every single shot. There are some laugh out loud moments but to me it was more of a none laughing comedy, that's not to say it's not funny, it is, it's just that you laugh inside more than outwardly as you identify with the uncomfortable situations. With little cameos from Bill Murray, Natalie Portman and Angelica Huston adding to the line-up the film gently travels along its path to hopefully enlightenment for the cast and audience alike. This won't be to everyone's taste, some will find it slow and too earnest, maybe even a little twee, but if you let yourself go along for the ride you should find the Darjeeling an interesting journey to take.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>The movie starts with a short film featuring Jason Schwartzman and Natalie Portman - it is well done but I could not see the point, maybe someone can explain that to me.So I will skip to Part II - this thankfully had no Natalie Portman, instead it started with Bill Murray on a madcap hair-raising taxi ride through the town of Jodhpur to the train station to catch the Darjeeling Limited! Owen has planned a trip for them to discover their filial feelings and bond as brothers. The madcap journey involves praying at various shrines, having sex with the attractive train hostess who delivers nimbu-pani (lemonade) and savory snacks, drinking lots of cough syrup, consuming heavy duty pain killers, and opiate pain killers dispensed through a dropper, pepper spray and a snake bought at the local bazaar, and engaging in some "mind cleansing" ritual with peacock feathers and chants! The snake escapes, the pepper spray is injudiciously used and the trio with their 14 or so suitcases, a printer and a laminating machine are thrown off the train. Now they are on their own in the desert, trundling their suitcases along and fall into another adventure that involves saving some drowning boys, but not all are saved. A funeral is attended and a sojourn in the village does finally result in the much sought after bonding between the brothers. It is in fact ironic that the brothers who are new to India achieve some measure of peace and acceptance of each other, while the mother (Anjelica Huston) who has run away to a convent in India, actually runs away again to escape from her progeny who are visiting her! The movie is exceedingly well done - the atmosphere is palpable, and one can almost smell the dust. India is shot au naturel with no glamor but is most appealing due to the personalities shown - some scintillating, some terse, others simply beautiful even when they do not speak much. One such personality is Irrfan Khan - he does not need words to convey his feeling in this film. Owen Wilson is genius as the rich, managing, conniving older brother wrapped up in bandages from his accidental encounter with a mountain. Adrien Brody is the funniest with that oh so serious face, and constantly wearing his dead Dad;s prescription sunglasses. Jason Schwartzman is excellent as the cool ladies-man younger brother who finally has to use the pepper spray! Most of the music is from Satyajit Ray films and a rare treat. The cinematography is brilliant with no gimmickry, the dialogs quirky and often hilarious, and the movie is infused with the mystical sense of a personal journey that seems like it is going nowhere until things finally click into place.A must watch for Wes Anderson fans but I think there is much here for all to like.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Darjeeling is very limited...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Before I say how much I disliked The Darjeeling Limited, let me start by saying that I consider Rushmore (1998) to be one of the more important films I have seen. However, since Rushmore and its predecessor, Bottle Rocket (1996), Wes Anderson has shown his audience very little to convince them that he's interested in growing as an artist. His recent films (The Life Aquatic with Steve Zissou (2001) and The Royal Tennenbaums (2004) , Anderson has been fixated largely portraying little more than spoiled and sheltered rich kids enacting affected and weak characters to heavy handed ends. While his first two films worked with themes very well (maybe the scripts were just better) his films since, have had little else to support them other than their evident quirkiness. His reliance on his childhood friends as a safety net to indulge him time after time despite the lack of palpable inspiration is regrettable. Is it too much to ask of a very talented director for a probing and mature evolution of his initial inspiration for film-making? Is it too much to ask of a very talented director for a probing and mature evolution of his initial inspiration for film-making? Professionally, one would maybe address the success of using his mostly ensemble cast to some triumph and then try something else after three or four films? Darjeeling has a great premise, very interesting actors and the stunning canvas of India to work with but all Wes could muster from it was a small collection of gags surrounding only a hint of actual family drama about three brothers whom I could hardly feel any empathy for. I didn't feel like there really was a death in the family and that the characters were just talking about it because it was in the script!Wes Anderson isn't here to duplicate the magic of Rushmore for me or any other disappointed film goers who loved his first two movies as much as I did, but it won't stop me from saying that I am embarrassed for him lately. He has devolved into a director who has left substance behind for style alone.In the interest of recognizing the diversity of public opinion let me add that when the film was over there were six or eight people who applauded.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>This movie makes me want to go to India. As in "The Royal Tenenbaums," he deals again with the dysfunctions and neuroses of family in a style reminiscent of J.D. Salinger, but with more humor. Here he tells the story of three brothers reuniting on a journey through India. The eldest calls it a "spiritual journey" but does it stay that way? You'll see.As usual Wes employs great music, indulging (maybe too much) in his trademark slow-motion scenes to carefully-selected songs. I especially enjoyed his usage of the Kinks "This Time Tomorrow." Joe Dassin's "Les Champs Elysees" was another nice touch, a tune I remember from 6th grade French. Personally I wanted more to be resolved in the end (which comes abruptly), but perhaps this was his intent.</fullReview>

Adrien Brody plays the disgruntled, still-grieving middle brother, Peter, who grudgingly came along for the ride. Jason Schwartzman plays the impatient youngest brother, Jack, who likes to turn his personal life into literature, and is seeking suitable alternatives to his recent break-up. The three come together on The Darjeeling Limited under the direction of Francis, and the story beautifully unfolds thereafter.</fullReview>

The saturated, zoom-lens styling is vintage 1960 ... but, wait, there's an MP3 player throughout ... so there are still people like this today? If you're part of that scene, or envious of it, you won't find it funny. Otherwise, the laughs are rooted in amazement. There's no conventional narrative because ? as a schematic about living a tunnel-vision life ? not even a real loss can register for three clueless Americans for more than a day. This is India as seen through their eyes. They learn a little something, but not much. Sad and funny at the same time.This is a Rorschach ... my favorite kind of movie.</fullReview>

		<username>zen23782</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>A movie with a soul.... and great music from The Kinks.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I believe that when you love a movie its because you see it at exactly the right time in your life to trigger a big emotional response. I'm 38 and this movie spoke volumes to me. What's it about? The plot for me is life itself... you start off with one plan and it ends up getting wrecked / rearranged by unforeseen occurrences and you're own lack ability to communicate properly with the people you love the most. Your intentions might be honourable but the results are often damaging. BUT&gt;.. that's not a bad thing. Because out of the chaos and misunderstanding that your plan generates something else emerges. Something you could never have planned out. Owen Wilsons character had it all mapped out but the behaviour of his brothers and his inability to tell them the real reason for the journey upfront results in them being thrown off the train... and that's where the real experience begins. Away from the itinerary... away from the known path and the tourist routes... in a place even the railroad hasn't got on its map. This movie is also an excellent mirror to hold up to any sibling relationship on the planet. The dynamics of being brothers (or sisters). Who gains the confidence of who? And how left out do you feel when someone close doesn't share information? On top of all this -the film is fantastic to look at and it made me want to see India for myself. I had no interest in going there before I sat down to watch the movie. So it succeeds in showing me something I'm aware of in a new light. It also manages to mix emotive subject matter with laugh out loud slapstick (When they descend the hill to board the train/when they fight over passports and run through the train). Finally - they used 3 or 4 tracks from the kinks album "lola vs powerman"... songs which are very cinematic and emotional. I love the kinks and so any film that draws attention to less well known songs by them is a good thing. "strangers" was written by Dave Davies about his brother Ray. They apparently used to fight like cat and dog and this echoes the theme of sibling difficulty in the movie...but also the sentiment of brotherhood that they all feel (lyric: for we are not two we are one). "this time tomorrow" from the same album is about constant movement and trying to plan the future at a time when you find it painful to look ahead at what's to come because deep down you haven't come to terms with the way things have recently changed. Again this echoes where the characters in the film are emotionally. Its 12 months since their dad died and the first year after a close death is often the hardest. Most of the time I find it frustrating when a film uses death as a dramatic foundation or turning point.... IE someone dies and everything changes in the story or the characters show more depth... but in this case it was entirely appropriate. Its real. This movie didn't depress me or leave me with any unanswered questions. A joy to watch and better (for me) than anything else the Directors done to date primarily because it got the job done in 91 mins rather than 120. It felt like quite a few things were stripped out before the final cut and that was most certainly a good thing.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-01-31</date>
		<username>Dorian Tenore-Bartilucci (dtb)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Brody, Wilson, and Schwartzman Make A Great Brother Act</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As an Adrien Brody fan, I have two ratings for his movies: "Worth seeing mostly because Adrien Brody happened to be in it" and "Would've loved it even if Adrien Brody hadn't been in it." THE DARJEELING LIMITED (TDL) is a little of both in the sense that TDL truly wouldn't have been the same without Brody, because he and Owen Wilson and Jason Schwartzman (who also wrote the great script with his cousin Roman Coppola and director Wes Anderson) have such perfect chemistry as the Whitman brothers: respectively, middle brother Peter, oldest brother Francis, and youngest brother Jack. TDJ also shows a rarely-seen side of Brody: his flair for comedy.At Francis's request, the estranged brothers meet in India, hoping to track down the mother who abandoned them and strengthen the brotherly bond that's been unraveling since their father's hit-and-run death. The Whitmans (Whitmen? :-)) find themselves (in more ways than one) having a series of oddball adventures through the wilds of fascinating, beautiful, hardship-ridden India, made all the wilder by Francis' well-meant but maddening control-freak tendencies (the man has his schedules laminated, for crying out loud!); Jack's fiction, which isn't as fictional as he claims it is (as a writer who's always getting family feedback, I liked the brothers' funny/poignant reactions to Jack's story); the brothers' lifelong expectation that everything will fall apart, including their relationships; and their tendencies to act, well, not exactly like Ugly Americans -- more like Dorky Americans as they get hung up on material possessions like Indian medicines, pet cobras, and the Louis Vuitton by Marc Jacobs luggage they inherited from their father in addition to all their emotional baggage (I wanted to cheer when they finally got rid of both).In addition to being believable as brothers, especially in their comical petty bickering, agreement-making, and secret-keeping early on, Wilson, Brody, and Schwartzman all look good in their own vaguely dorky way (that's meant as a compliment, I assure you :-)). All three of them have big ol' sad-puppy eyes; as my husband and I have joked, a good alternate title for TDL would be "THE 'AWWW!' MOVIE." :-) (As Peter, Brody's eyes are often hidden behind the oversize sunglasses he inherited from their late dad, making him look like a 1960s European movie star.) I wouldn't complain if the three of them teamed up for another film together, with or without Anderson (but preferably with Anderson, since everyone worked so well together)! Much has been said about how, after Wilson's real-life suicide attempt, seeing his head bandaged up for most of the film from what turns out to be a suicide attempt becomes doubly poignant, and I can't disagree, though I think TDL would still have emotional resonance without it. Watch for fun bits by other members of Anderson's repertory company, including Bill Murray as a tourist in a hurry and Anjelica Huston as the Whitman brothers' runaway mom.When TDL opened wide, Fox Searchlight attached HOTEL CHEVALIER (HC), the "prequel" short previously available online, to TDL as "Part 1" of the story (TDL being "Part 2," of course), giving us moviegoers two movies for the price of one. The episode portrays a tryst between Jack Whitman and the unnamed ex (Natalie Portman) he can't stay away from, with Peter Sarstedt's wistful song "Where Do You Go To (My Lovely)?" playing in the background. (This song also plays in TDL when Jack is pitching woo at Rita, played appealingly by Rosario Dawson lookalike Amara Karan.) Schwartzman and Portman have a chemistry that's prickly yet sexy and wistful. Portman gives one of her best performances here; she's like Audrey Hepburn possessed by the world-weary spirit of Simone Signoret or Jeanne Moreau. I half-expected Portman to choke on that toothpick she always had in her mouth, though! Anyway, HC has a sort of yearning charm, and it looked like it was fun to film, especially the love scene. :-) As always, Anderson uses music well. In particular, TDL uses great songs by Anderson's fave band The Kinks, as well as lots of stirring music from the films of Satyajit Ray. I must admit I never knew Indian music could make such a good backdrop for chase/action scenes. TDL is the dysfunctional family comedy-drama of the year, and Wes Anderson's best film since RUSHMORE, for my money. Rent or buy the DVD when it comes out in February!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>what a lovely, lovely film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The film is bleached to the ruddy-yellows of turmeric, the color of curry, and to the color of saffron. It gives a dreamy quality to the thing... yellow's not a pretty or a soothing color, but there is a spiritual openness, a sun-bakedness, about it here. We occasionally get to balm our eyes in deep indigo for contrast and rest.This spirit quest is dreamlike, the men are plucked from their lives and placed on this flimsy, beautiful train, which rocks and sways as it cuts slowly across the savanna. It's a command performance called by the oldest brother who has had an epiphany, and there are some things to work out (that's about all he knows about it), and it will take all three.The dry humor of interaction that calls back to their history as brothers- which we are wisely left to intuit and guess at ourselves- is delicious and pleasing. Just the right notes are hit here.. we understand just enough of who these men, and these boys, are.It's a delight to watch the spirit of the quest move into and animate the brothers; to watch the playing out of old patterns, the play of old hurts and loves, the reaching back and reaching forward, the odd increase in both humility and surety that comes with spiritual growth.The music is exactly what it should be- intelligent, sensual, dreamy.The flat-on snapshot like compositions of the three brothers are interesting and effective.The picture moves well, and resolves well. It is sensual and touching and funny. It is beautifully shot. The players are well suited to their roles, and play off each other very very well. The extraneous characters and scenes are not extraneous in any way, they complement and complete..An absolute delight.</fullReview>

The exquisite soundtrack combines modern tunes with traditional Indian song, while the colours, chaos and spirituality of India make this one of the most intensely stunning pictures I have seen all year. 		<shortDescription>The Magical Indian Tour</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I'm a fan of Wes Anderson's previous works, and I have to admit that I was shocked to see Adrien Brody and Jason Schwartzmann as Owen Wilson's brothers at first. But it's all part of the plan - they don't look alike in any aspect, and the gigantic difference in height between the three is proof that Wes probably didn't want your 'typical' brothers. Kinda like the "Three Amigos". Their differences make them funny. The shorter guy is the most seductive one, the taller is a quiet man who's about to be a dad, and Owen Wilson really shines here as a borderline-nut case with a lot of heart. The director uses a lot of intentional repetition in different scenes which makes the viewer feel attached to the characters, almost as if you were one of them, and that's fun.The story is basically about these 3 brothers who go travel across India on a train (The Darjeeling Limited) on a 'spiritual journey'. Their goals are not certain and their agendas are not the same, but this is a case of a simple story well-told.I can't say enough about the quality of his film. The camera shots make it look like you're inside the train with them. There's a particular telepathy scene that is marvelous. The colors are like the ones you'd experience in a psychedelic trip, and the scenery is fantastic. The soundtrack is excellent as well, it really transports you into their world. This is AWESOME film-making.It's a different movie from all of Wes' previous works, but I can see he drew a bit from Bottle Rocket, Tenembaums and The Life Aquatic. Darjeeling's Owen Wilson has the same obsession with itineraries as in Bottle Rocket, the family theme is borrowed from the Tenembaums and etc. Basically, his themes haven't changed - they've been improved, and his storytelling technique is now more brilliant than ever. Be sure to check out the short "Hotel Chevalier", as it is great in its own right and tied to this film. This movie proves there are still very talented people in the business.9/10</fullReview>

In fact, it would be easy to believe that the man faked us all out as a publicity stunt for this otherwise bland and unworthy movie - make that film, rather: the sort of filmy thing that will put most of us to sleep while critics credit its "ambiguity" as some higher form of humor. The kind where you put a finger to your cheek and say "Yes, I recognize that the underpinning of that line had a comic intent" instead of actually laughing out loud.If pretty pictures are the end-all, be-all, this movie has a few of those and presumably we are looking at some actual India locations. But be warned even on that score: most of the movie takes place on the train or on interior sets that could have been shot anywhere.It would be easy to sum up the movie as "the reason Owen Wilson did it," but despite its self-congratulatory tone and flat-out boring end result at least Owen doesn't have a screenplay credit on this one so he should take some comfort in that.It feels like a first draft script without a pass for clarity and comic inspiration. Boring pretty much sums it up. It settles for patting itself on the head for even thinking about anything reflective in Hollywood. Very shallow critics will love it. Real people will be better advised to look for something more fun. Even Wes Andersons previous efforts will do.</fullReview>

	</review>
	<review>
		<date>2007-09-07</date>
		<username>rehman-amer</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Style over substance and a great cast wasted....</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Just saw this, plus the prologue Hotel Chevalier, at the Venice Festival.Don't get me wrong, it's not a bad film, it just seems like an opportunity missed. Anderson makes beautiful, intelligent films with complex characters. However, while all these elements are here, the story itself is too thin to pull it off.The ingredients are all there - three brothers embarking on a train and, hopefully spiritual, journey in India together. Owen Wilson is consummately intelligent and kooky as ever, Adrien Brody does what he can with the material but Jason Schwartzman really comes of age as an adult actor. The cinematography is fantastic (although as any Bollywood watcher will know, it's hard NOT to film India beautifully) and there are several iconic use of images and colours, not least the fantastically retro Louis Vuitton luggage, and some nice character and family quirks tenderly observed; but the film frustrates in only touching at the obvious depth of the main characters, and hence their motives. Thus it's hard to maintain interest in their denouement.The film is saved from superficiality mainly by use powerful scenes with local actors, participial the superb Irfan Khan, who demonstrates his skills again after his landmark turn in 'The Namesake', ironically a lesson to Anderson in the power of a great story. Amara Karan also is great support, let down only by her English accent slipping through.Worth a watch, not just for the expected occasional great lines and the heavyweight talent on show, but one can't help but feel that Anderson needs to leave this genre behind and show the world his talents more accessibly, perhaps by using actors he hasn't grown up with.Overall, a great idea, too lightly executed - these characters would have made a great novel, but 2 hours in a cinema doesn't do it justice.</fullReview>
	</review>
</movie>
